  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }

      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app',

        state: 'apple_oauth_state_29644784-e3c5-4311-b66b-e7a16330f2ea'

      <div id="description" class="readable stacked" style="right:0">
        
<span id="freeTextContainer11155484379117029171">Beginning on August 9, 1945, in Nagasaki, and ending in a prison cell in the US in 2002, as a man is waiting to be sent to Guantanamo Bay, Burnt Shadows is an epic narrative of love and betrayal.<br/><br/>Hiroko Tanaka is twenty-one and in love with the man she is to marry, Konrad Weiss. As she steps onto her veranda, wrapped in a kimono with three black cranes swooping across the b</span>
  <span id="freeText11155484379117029171" style="display:none">Beginning on August 9, 1945, in Nagasaki, and ending in a prison cell in the US in 2002, as a man is waiting to be sent to Guantanamo Bay, Burnt Shadows is an epic narrative of love and betrayal.<br /><br />Hiroko Tanaka is twenty-one and in love with the man she is to marry, Konrad Weiss. As she steps onto her veranda, wrapped in a kimono with three black cranes swooping across the back, her world is suddenly and irrevocably altered. In the numbing aftermath of the atomic bomb that obliterates everything she has known, all that remains are the bird-shaped burns on her back, an indelible reminder of the world she has lost. In search of new beginnings, two years later, Hiroko travels to Delhi. It is there that her life will become intertwined with that of Konrad's half sister, Elizabeth, her husband, James Burton, and their employee Sajjad Ashraf, from whom she starts to learn Urdu. <br /><br />With the partition of India, and the creation of Pakistan, Hiroko will find herself displaced once again, in a world where old wars are replaced by new conflicts. But the shadows of history--personal and political--are cast over the interrelated worlds of the Burtons, the Ashrafs, and the Tanakas as they are transported from Pakistan to New York and, in the novel's astonishing climax, to Afghanistan in the immediate wake of 9/11. The ties that have bound these families together over decades and generations are tested to the extreme, with unforeseeable consequences.</span>
  <a data-text-id="11155484379117029171" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

      </div>
</div>

          $$('div.prototip').each(function(i){i.setStyle({zIndex: $('box').getStyle('z-index')})});
        } else {
          $$('div.prototip').each(function(i){i.setStyle({zIndex: 6000})});
        }
      });

      newTip['wrapper'].addClassName('prototipAllowOverflow');

        $('edition_11072200').observe('prototip:shown', function () {
          $$('div.prototip').each(function (e) {
            if ($('edition_11072200').hasClassName('ignored')) {
              e.setStyle({'display': 'none'});
              return;
            }
            e.setStyle({'overflow': 'visible'});
          });
        });

  <div class="friendReviews elementListBrown" >
    <div class="section firstReview">
      

<div id="review_834994626" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/834994626" />
    <a title="Paul" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4643325-paul"><img alt="Paul" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1535048395p2/4643325.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/834994626?book_show_action=true">Jan 26, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Paul" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Paul" href="/user/show/4643325-paul">Paul</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4643325-paul?shelf=modern-classics">modern-classics</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer834994626" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6402500746161520414">A novel with great scope ranging over a vast sweep of modern history, written with great warmth and understanding. The characters are well drawn and believable. Characters with flaws, who make mistakes which have consequences, but who are understandable and feel like real people.<br/>The novel is broken up into three sections. The first is in the 1940s; in 1945 Hiroko Tanaka has become engaged to Konrad Weiss, a German living, like her in Nagasaki. He is killed by the atomic bomb and she is injured. </span>
  <span id="freeText6402500746161520414" style="display:none">A novel with great scope ranging over a vast sweep of modern history, written with great warmth and understanding. The characters are well drawn and believable. Characters with flaws, who make mistakes which have consequences, but who are understandable and feel like real people.<br />The novel is broken up into three sections. The first is in the 1940s; in 1945 Hiroko Tanaka has become engaged to Konrad Weiss, a German living, like her in Nagasaki. He is killed by the atomic bomb and she is injured. Following her recovery she goes (in 1947) to India to see Konrad’s sister Ilse (Elizabeth), who is married to an Englishman, James Burton who is a lawyer and rather upper middle class. This coincides with the end of English rule and partition. Hiroko meets one of James Burton’s employees Sajjad Ashraf. They fall in love and marry; partition taking them from Dehli to Karachi. <br />The second part moves to Pakistan in the early 1980s and the backdrop is the struggle against the Soviets in Afghanistan. The story here revolves around Sajjad and Hiroko again, their teenage son Raza and James and Ilse’s son Harry, who is working for the CIA (Ilse has left James and is now American). <br />The final section is set just after 9/11. Harry and Raza are working together in Afghanistan for a private security company, whilst Ilse and Hioko are in New York. Harry’s daughter Kim plays a central role. <br />A novel which seeks to encompass the dropping of the atomic bomb, the end of empire, partition, class, the cold war, the CIA supporting the mujahedeen, 9/11, the Taliban, Guantanamo Bay, terrorism, the fear of terrorism and the war in Afghanistan sets its sights high. The themes are no less impressive love, family, trust and betrayal, friendship, religion and a clash of cultures. <br />On the whole it works rather well; intelligently written, compassionate, gripping Shamsie attempts to explain some of recent history’s more complex issues through a family saga. A couple of grumbles; the ending is flagged up too early and obviously and it really is too short. Many hefty books would benefit from being shorter. I felt this one would have stood being longer to develop the issues and some of the characters. <br />Those are minor points; this is a competent and compassionate examination of many of the issues the modern world has to grapple with and I would recommend it.<br />4.5 stars</span>
  <a data-text-id="6402500746161520414" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/49365462-w?shelf=pakistani-fiction">pakistani-fiction</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2043269973" class="readable"

<span id="freeTextContainer5300201745273182697">For me,it is Kamila Shamsie's second best book,after Home Fire.Not that I'd call it a great book,but it's still much better than the rest of her books.<br/><br/>It tries to tackle too many subjects,and doesn't quite succeed.It begins with the story of a Japanese victim of the atomic bombing of Nagasaki.<br/><br/>But if the idea was to show the destructive effects of the atomic bomb on this victim,that doesn't happen.The Japanese woman moves to India and goes through the rest of her life, without showing any ill eff</span>
  <span id="freeText5300201745273182697" style="display:none">For me,it is Kamila Shamsie's second best book,after Home Fire.Not that I'd call it a great book,but it's still much better than the rest of her books.<br /><br />It tries to tackle too many subjects,and doesn't quite succeed.It begins with the story of a Japanese victim of the atomic bombing of Nagasaki.<br /><br />But if the idea was to show the destructive effects of the atomic bomb on this victim,that doesn't happen.The Japanese woman moves to India and goes through the rest of her life, without showing any ill effects.<br /><br />The book then goes on to include a pet peeve of Kamila Shamsie, the partition of India and the creation of Pakistan.It is a constant theme in her work as her own family had to move to Pakistan at the time of partition.<br /><br />Then,there are detailed descriptions of life in Karachi.It helps to fill up the pages and is also part of several of her other books.<br /><br />After that,she returns to international conflict once again.First comes the Soviet invasion of Afghanistan,and a Karachi young man's journey to fight the Soviets.This whole section is very superficial.Added for good measure is the story of a US agent and his daughter as well.<br /><br />Then comes the US invasion of Afghanistan,and that is included,as well.Again,nothing special about this part of the book.<br /><br />In between,the subcontinent becomes nuclearised in 1998 after India's nuclear tests and Pakistan following suit.So,the Japanese woman,already a victim of the nuclear bomb, gets scared and moves to New York.<br /><br />While some parts of the book were interesting,at times it felt very contrived,particularly the portion on Afghanistan.<br /><br />The last hundred pages,in particular were so boring that I was relieved when it finally came to an end.A good many of the situations in the book,are scarcely believeable.<br /><br />Too many conflicts are included,without focusing effectively on any one of them.The book essentially lacks a coherent storyline.</span>

<div id="review_80890915" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/80890915" />
    <a title="Barbara H" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1883816-barbara-h"><img alt="Barbara H" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1572546566p2/1883816.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/80890915?book_show_action=true">Dec 13, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Barbara H" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Barbara H" href="/user/show/1883816-barbara-h">Barbara H</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780312551872" href="/book/show/5997336-burnt-shadows">review of another edition</a>

          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">Merilee,Maria, Gail</span></div>

          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Barbara H by: </span>
      Rose O (with gratitude)
  </div>


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1883816-barbara-h?shelf=holocaust-ww-2">holocaust-ww-2</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1883816-barbara-h?shelf=asia">asia</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer80890915" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2692103224979556105">"Burnt Shadows" was a gift to me from a friend who valued this book highly. It was a gift for me because it has given me much food for thought. At the outset, I was determined to enjoy this book to share the pleasure with my friend, but as I progressed I could observe why she ranked it so extremely well. <br/><br/>I will not attempt a summary here. One can easily find that elsewhere. The scope of this novel is huge. It spans about 60 years, from the A- Bomb in Nagasaki, to the partitioning of India and Pa</span>
  <span id="freeText2692103224979556105" style="display:none">"Burnt Shadows" was a gift to me from a friend who valued this book highly. It was a gift for me because it has given me much food for thought. At the outset, I was determined to enjoy this book to share the pleasure with my friend, but as I progressed I could observe why she ranked it so extremely well. <br /><br />I will not attempt a summary here. One can easily find that elsewhere. The scope of this novel is huge. It spans about 60 years, from the A- Bomb in Nagasaki, to the partitioning of India and Pakistan, to wartorn Afghanistan and to post 9/11 America. Shamsie has written a profound and powerful novel of family, nations and wars. Throughout this book we see the power of language, not solely as the means for communication, but as a cultural and attitudinal divide.<br /><br /> Shamsie has deftly described scenes of racism, class division and the panorama of wars. There are wars for power, wars for religion, wars for territory or family. But what is the outcome for the people who have suffered at the hands of their invaders? In a conversation with an American woman, an Afghan man who had fought against the Soviets stated, "...countries like yours they always fight wars, but always somewhere else. The disease always happens somewhere else.It's why you fight more wars than anyone else; because you understand war least of all." <br /><br />In response to an act of apparent racial profiling, another character equates this with the concentration camps and replies, "...you have to deny people their humanity in order to decimate them." Shamsie has expertly drawn her characters and left the reader with a clear sense of them.<br /><br />It has been difficult to encapsulate this novel into a short review because it is so complex and extensive. It has made a deep impact on me, with emotions ranging from tears and sorrow to anger.</span>
  <a data-text-id="2692103224979556105" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_770366145" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/770366145" />
    <a title="Zanna" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/22591633-zanna"><img alt="Zanna" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1512813535p2/22591633.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/770366145?book_show_action=true">Oct 04, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Zanna" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Zanna" href="/user/show/22591633-zanna">Zanna</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780312551872" href="/book/show/5997336-burnt-shadows">review of another edition</a>


          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Zanna by: </span>
      <a href="/user/show/17129113-alice">Alice</a>
  </div>


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer770366145" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2927042868130763809"><blockquote>'Why? Can't women travel alone in India?'<br/><br/>Elizabeth almost laughed. So much for those demure Japanese women of all the stories she'd heard. Here was one who would squeeze the sun in her fist if she ever got the chance; yes, and tilt her head back to swallow its liquid light.</blockquote>Here is how to write a novel with emotional truth: find your characters, and let them make their stories. But Kamila Shamsie, who in Burnt Shadows does this so superbly, has done so much more. When I shared the opening themes </span>
  <span id="freeText2927042868130763809" style="display:none"><blockquote>'Why? Can't women travel alone in India?'<br /><br />Elizabeth almost laughed. So much for those demure Japanese women of all the stories she'd heard. Here was one who would squeeze the sun in her fist if she ever got the chance; yes, and tilt her head back to swallow its liquid light.</blockquote>Here is how to write a novel with emotional truth: find your characters, and let them make their stories. But Kamila Shamsie, who in Burnt Shadows does this so superbly, has done so much more. When I shared the opening themes of the book with my brother, who lives in Japan (I will spare readers of this review a lengthy account of all the many borders this story crossed into my own life), said, referring to the bombing of Nagasaki <i>art is essential to capture such feelings</i>. Even to call it essential doesn't do justice to this text, which covers such an immense ground, making a kind of sense of the world that has been terrifyingly coming into being since 1945 from the perspective of what one character, with fatal disdain, calls <i>ordinary, little-picture morality</i>, the spider's eye view, people and their relationships...<br /><br />So many novels make their protagonists suffer, but Shamsie gives Hiroko, as if to atone for making her witness the bomb, for making her lose so much, carry so much, not only competence, talent, boldness among many admirable qualities, an independent spirit (<blockquote>it didn't bother her in the least to know she would always be a foreigner in Pakistan - she had no interest in belonging to anything as contradictorily insubstantial and damaging as a nation</blockquote>) and ambiguously generous benefactors, but an extraordinarily desirable opposite number, countless sources of delight written and unwritten, a son who earns his own story, and always the last word. Frequently, in other characters' strands, we are made aware of barriers raised against women, but Hiroko always knows exactly which boundary is worthy of respect, which border may be politely or heedlessly transgressed.<br /><br />Language lessons and translation are the ropes, ladders, hooks, bridges of the story. In learning language, the learner thinks their way into a cultural borderland where all manner of connections are possible, where influences mix, and from where their own culture looks different. The willingness, or otherwise, to hear and understand and learn another's language, or to teach, signals the direction of a story, an emotion. James Burton tells Hiroko it's not necessary for her to learn Urdu. Kim tells an Afghan Muslim she has read the Quran in English (how often do Muslims hear this?), while he, she presumes, has never read it in a language he understands, therefore she knows better than he does what it contains. Meanwhile, Hiroko only teaches Sajjad expressions of love in Japanese, Raza enters Iran and translates flirtatious questions of the man driving him to a carful of women into Farsi, having learned it while working in Dubai. 'Why are you travelling by car?' the driver wants to know, 'don't angels fly?' but they laughingly reply that women are superior to angels. <br /><br />For all their noble feelings, their kind gestures, and their good intentions, the English and USians in this novel are treacherous, and these portrayals, especially, to me, of the Burtons, rang absolutely true. What was beautiful, soothing, redemptive to me in this story, was the view from elsewhere, from Hiroko and her birds, from Konrad and his optimism, from Sajjad and his family, from Raza and his friends. It doesn't have to be this way... there is no possible justification for the second bomb in a picture of any size. Things can change. Not everyone is like <i>us</i>.<blockquote>'James!' Elizabeth said, coming to stand beside her husband. 'Did you know Sajjad's family came here from Turkey seven centuries ago?'<br /><br />'Young Turk are you?' James smiled at Sajjad.<br /><br />'No, Mr Burton,' Sajjad said, not understanding the reference. 'I'm Indian.' He glanced at Hiroko, who had her back to the three of them, looking up at the Arabic inscriptions on the minaret. She was offended, he knew, but what could he do about it? He looked at James, as though considering something that had never occurred to him before. 'Why have the English remained so English? Throughout India's history conquerors have come from elsewhere, and all of them - Turk, Arab, Hun, Mongol, Persian - have become Indian. If - when - this Pakistan happens, those Muslims who leave Delhi and Lucknow and Hyderabad to go there, they will be leaving their homes. But when the English leave, they'll be going home.'<br /><br />Hiroko turned towards Sajjad, surprised and accutely self-conscious. She had been speaking to him of Konrad's interest in the foreigners who made their homes in Nagasaki, and now she saw her words filtering into his thoughts and becoming part of the way he saw the world.<br /><br />'Henry thinks of India as home,', Elizabeth said, seeing how wounded James was by Sajjad's unexpected attack, and wanting to deflect it.<br /><br />'Yes.' There was a tightening of Sajjad's voice. 'He does.' And you sent him away because of it, he wanted to say, the sense of offence which had started as an act to impress Hiroko no longer feigned. He recalled it very well, the day her opposition to the idea of boarding school ended. He had been playing cricket in the garden with Henry when Elizabeth came out and told her son he was 'such a young Englishman'. Henry had scowled, and backed up towards Sajjad. 'I'm Indian,' he'd said. The next day James Burton had told Sajjad how relieved he was that his wife had suddenly decided to withdraw all her 'sentimental' objections to sending Henry to boarding school.<br /><br />'Something you want to say, Sajjad?'<br /><br />'No, Mr Burton. Only that I don't suppose he'll continue to think of India that way for much longer.'<br /><br />'For the best,' Elizabeth said, looking around her, feeling something that was almost sorrow to think that the descendants of the English would not come to the churches and monuments of British India seven centuries from now and say this is a reminder of when my family history and India's history entered the same stream irrevocably and for ever.<br /><br />'Why is it for the best?' Sajjad's voice was as near angry as anyone had ever heard it. It was hard to say if Elizabeth or Sajjad was more surprised at his tone after eight years during which he used only excessive politeness as a weapon against her. But they were both aware that this would not have happened if Hiroko hadn't been there, disrupting all hierarchies.</blockquote></span>
  <a data-text-id="2927042868130763809" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_109634414" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/109634414" />
    <a title="Elizabeth" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5-elizabeth"><img alt="Elizabeth" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1169594940p2/5.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/109634414?book_show_action=true">Jul 01, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Elizabeth" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Elizabeth" href="/user/show/5-elizabeth">Elizabeth</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781408804278" href="/book/show/6489706-burnt-shadows">review of another edition</a>


          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Elizabeth by: </span>
      <a href="/user/show/216786-ken-ichi">Ken-ichi</a>
  </div>


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5-elizabeth?shelf=india">india</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5-elizabeth?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5-elizabeth?shelf=europe">europe</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5-elizabeth?shelf=new-york">new-york</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5-elizabeth?shelf=japan">japan</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5-elizabeth?shelf=asia">asia</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5-elizabeth?shelf=afghanistan">afghanistan</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer109634414" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3296562668555770600">A twisting yarn of a book that struck me as something written fresh on the heels of 9-11. There were certain elements of the plot that I thought were probably even more impactful for readers who read this book a few years after that horrific event. <br/><br/>Beginning in Nagasaki, Japan, just before the second nuclear bomb drops, the story ventures to India, Turkey, Pakistan, and New York as it follows two families, one of German-English and another Japanese-Pakistani extraction. Lives mirror and intersec</span>
  <span id="freeText3296562668555770600" style="display:none">A twisting yarn of a book that struck me as something written fresh on the heels of 9-11. There were certain elements of the plot that I thought were probably even more impactful for readers who read this book a few years after that horrific event. <br /><br />Beginning in Nagasaki, Japan, just before the second nuclear bomb drops, the story ventures to India, Turkey, Pakistan, and New York as it follows two families, one of German-English and another Japanese-Pakistani extraction. Lives mirror and intersect, and Shamsie makes some important observations about class and racial tension. <br /><br />Because this book was recommended to me for my fondness for The Far Pavilions, I expected a grand, sweeping love story. No luck. The romance motif in this book is nothing like Ashok's fairy-tales. Instead the relationships are broken down jalopies, bravely forging ahead on unstable ground. On that negative note, one could also easily compare this novel to The Kite Runner for it's ties to the conflict in Afghanistan. For me, the Kite Runner is more straight-forwardly depressing. This book is much more thoughtful and introspective. <br /><br />Ultimately, what was most interesting to me was the cultural soup of Japanese, German, British, Indian, Pakistani, and Americans that flavored the novel. My general takeaway was that the author wanted to show what happens when people choose to be nice to "the other", but still not treat them as equals--those actions have far-reaching, inhumane, humiliating consequences. I also thought that it was interesting to note that author dismisses the idea of using the personal element of a tragedy as the centerpiece of a larger tragedy...<br /><br />Food for thought (Regarding Nagasaki):<br /><br />"You lived it," Kim said. "our father died in it. Your fiance died in it. There's no shame in putting all the weight in the world on that." <br />It was the wrong answer.<br />Hiroko turned to her, face bright with anger. <br />"Is that why? That's why Nagasaki was such a monstrous crime? Because it happened to me?" She pulled the gloves off and threw them at Kim. "I don't want your hot chocolate," she said and stalked away.<br /><br />This book brings up a lot of sediment to sift through. It wasn't always pleasant to read, but it <i>was</i> educational. <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="3296562668555770600" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_3149720947" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3149720947" />
    <a title="Roman Clodia" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/56111743-roman-clodia"><img alt="Roman Clodia" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1464021031p2/56111743.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3149720947?book_show_action=true">Jan 22, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Roman Clodia" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Roman Clodia" href="/user/show/56111743-roman-clodia">Roman Clodia</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781408804278" href="/book/show/6489706-burnt-shadows">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3149720947" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17416812719484446923">For the first two-thirds of this book it was 5-stars in my head, up there with <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/33621427.Home_Fire' title='Home Fire by Kamila Shamsie' rel='nofollow noopener'>Home Fire</a> - then the final third just became <i>too</i> convenient, <i>too</i> schematic, <i>too</i> obviously plotted. I could believe that someone present in Nagasaki the day the bomb was dropped could take herself to India to witness the dying days of the Raj, then the Partition and birth of Pakistan. I could even accept that her teenage son should become embroiled in the politics of Afghanistan when the Soviets invade, given his mixed</span>
  <span id="freeText17416812719484446923" style="display:none">For the first two-thirds of this book it was 5-stars in my head, up there with <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/33621427.Home_Fire" title="Home Fire by Kamila Shamsie" rel="nofollow noopener">Home Fire</a> - then the final third just became <i>too</i> convenient, <i>too</i> schematic, <i>too</i> obviously plotted. I could believe that someone present in Nagasaki the day the bomb was dropped could take herself to India to witness the dying days of the Raj, then the Partition and birth of Pakistan. I could even accept that her teenage son should become embroiled in the politics of Afghanistan when the Soviets invade, given his mixed heritage and the way it manifests in his features. But when the same two intertwined families <i>also</i> implicate themselves in post-9/11 Afghanistan, it all becomes a step too contrived - and even more unbelievable given their ages by then <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[an active CIA operative who is 65 working in Afghanistan? <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span>.<br /><br />It's a shame Shamsie's judgement fails her towards the end because much of this book is just so good. There are images that are shocking: <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[ the birds-burns on Hiroko's back that char the past into her flesh, the horrific last sight of her father <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span>, but also a love and marriage that is deep and nuanced. Shamsie is especially good at writing scenes that are emotive without ever falling into sentimentalism. <br /><br />She also manages the sweep of the book very well: as we move between sections, she fills in the gaps without recourse to clumsy narrative - though, at the same time, there are stories that take place which we never witness. For example, I wanted to know more, much more, about how Hiroko and Sajjad came to Pakistan, lived in a refugee camp, before we pick up their story, years later, in a middle class neighbourhood.<br /><br />I'd say with this book we can see the writer who will emerge so triumphantly in Home Fire coming into being. This novel is still a bit too diffuse, lacking that targeted intensity of the later piece. Still, for the mingling of a family story with politics, this is very good. It's hard to write a female character who is strong and a bit abrasive yet who is also soft and loving, but Shamsie does it brilliantly with Hiroko. Overall, I'd say this is a not-quite-there-yet book, but one which gives a human face to big, global political movements. </span>
  <a data-text-id="17416812719484446923" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_345627816" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/345627816" />
    <a title="Caroline" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/6257531-caroline"><img alt="Caroline" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1329765864p2/6257531.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/345627816?book_show_action=true">Jun 10, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Caroline" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Caroline" href="/user/show/6257531-caroline">Caroline</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6257531-caroline?shelf=4-star-reads">4-star-reads</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6257531-caroline?shelf=novels">novels</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer345627816" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12279053182953122576">I was impressed with the scope of this novel - from Japan to Pakistan to America, and covering about half a century - it touched upon a broad spectrum of cultures, politics and lives, with the twists and turns in the story largely governed by geographic location. <br/><br/>For me it was all about identity, and how a sense of identity can be damaged by the horror of an atomic bomb, or by failing exams, by subterfuge, or by looking different to those around you. But as well as exploring alienation, this boo</span>
  <span id="freeText12279053182953122576" style="display:none">I was impressed with the scope of this novel - from Japan to Pakistan to America, and covering about half a century - it touched upon a broad spectrum of cultures, politics and lives, with the twists and turns in the story largely governed by geographic location. <br /><br />For me it was all about identity, and how a sense of identity can be damaged by the horror of an atomic bomb, or by failing exams, by subterfuge, or by looking different to those around you. But as well as exploring alienation, this book explores the wonderful ties that bind people together. The loves, the loyalties and the unavoidable ambivalence that makes up the knots that keep us together.<br /><br />Several people in this book have the world opened up to them via their extraordinary gifts with languages, and reading this as a monoglot I was extremely envious. This is my fantasy, an effortless learning whereby new languages simply fall into your lap, and you are able to take off to new lands or explore new cultures, with a vast chunk of you being already at home in these new places. Friends teaching friends, mother teaching son, seemingly almost by osmosis. Yeah, I was envious.<br /><br />I also liked the fact that I learnt a lot from this book. We didn’t spend enough time in any one culture to be utterly immersed in it, but we touched upon some fascinating experiences – what it was like to be a German in Japan during the second world war, what it was like to be an educated Indian servant to a British family, what it was like to be in Pakistan and Afghanistan - supporting the mujahidean, what it was like to be on the run (both in Afghanistan and America), and what it was like to be a Muslim in New York after 9/11. It was most rewarding to have these experiences opened up to me.<br /><br />My one criticism is that some of the relationships felt a bit forced, almost too good to be true, also sometimes people’s lives seemed to take an unnecessary negative twist....almost on a whim. I felt like jogging the author’s elbow a couple of times. Having said that, most of the people in this book were beautifully evoked, their characters drawn with exquisite sensitivity, often via dialogue. All in all I found this book both moving and gripping. An excellent read.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="12279053182953122576" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="friendReviews elementListBrown" >
    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_412888332" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/412888332" />
    <a title="Anum S." class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/10920641-anum-s"><img alt="Anum S." src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1443257896p2/10920641.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/412888332?book_show_action=true">Sep 12, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Anum S." class="user" itemprop="url" name="Anum S." href="/user/show/10920641-anum-s">Anum S.</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/10920641-anum-s?shelf=muslim-characters">muslim-characters</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/10920641-anum-s?shelf=pakistani">pakistani</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/10920641-anum-s?shelf=books-everyone-should-read">books-everyone-should-read</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/10920641-anum-s?shelf=reviewed-in-detail">reviewed-in-detail</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/10920641-anum-s?shelf=loads-of-stuff-to-discuss">loads-of-stuff-to-discuss</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/10920641-anum-s?shelf=mind-doesn-t-know-what-to-think">mind-doesn-t-know-what-to-think</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/10920641-anum-s?shelf=asian-authors">asian-authors</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer412888332" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer851931862918342968"><i>She had not thought of destination so much as departure, wheeling through the world with the awful freedom of someone with no one to answer to. She had become, in fact, a figure out of myth. The character who loses everything and is born anew in blood. </i><br/><br/>This book is <b>amazing</b>. No, scratch that. Amazing is probably too weak a word here. Think Astounding. <b>Remarkable.</b> The kind of book you tell your best friend to read so you can discuss it together, going over all the finer points. <br/><br/>I’ll admit, I start</span>
  <span id="freeText851931862918342968" style="display:none"><i>She had not thought of destination so much as departure, wheeling through the world with the awful freedom of someone with no one to answer to. She had become, in fact, a figure out of myth. The character who loses everything and is born anew in blood. </i><br /><br />This book is <b>amazing</b>. No, scratch that. Amazing is probably too weak a word here. Think Astounding. <b>Remarkable.</b> The kind of book you tell your best friend to read so you can discuss it together, going over all the finer points. <br /><br />I’ll admit, I started the book with wary suspicions. Look at that blurb. Nagasaki and the atomic bomb. 1947 and partition. 2001 and New York City and Afghanistan. <b>The book looks like a mess waiting to happen, but.</b> It wasn’t. In fact, it was quite miraculous how everything came together. How the lives intertwined. I have used that word quite often, but nowhere does it fit more perfectly than in this book: intertwining of fate and coincidences and relationships is a big theme in this book, and <b>Kamila Shamsie’s canvas is the world. </b><br /><br />But let us start at the beginning.<br /><br /><b>The Summary</b><br /><br /><i> Yes, I know everything can disappear in a flash of light. That doesn’t make anything less valuable.</i><br /><br />It’s August 9, 1945, and Hiroko Tanaka, the disgraced daughter of a father who dared question the morality of children involved in Kamikaze flights, is very much in love, but with the wrong person: Konrad Weiss, a German living in Nagasaki, trying to understand their culture. In this instance, we know the bomb is going to fall, but what is more interesting is the xenophobia, the patriotism, the secrecy and the betrayals. Shamsie makes us care more about Hiroko’s mother’s death, about Konrad’s disloyal friend, about their secret love affair, than the devastation that we know is about to occur.<br /><br /><i>Since her mother’s death, she had taken to interpreting the silence from her father as an absence of anything worth communicating rather than an inability to form a new configuration with his daughter now his beloved wife is no longer around to serve as the voice to his thoughts.</i><br /><br />That’s not to say that the bomb is just background noise. No,<b> the bomb changes things, but we are now at the heart of those who suffered through it.</b> There is no fine distance, no aloofness to be hidden behind. Shamsie is not concerned with talking about governments and politics; she wants you to look at the human who lives through war time, who survives, who has to learn to pick up the pieces later.<br /><br /><i>“When the war’s over, I’ll be kind.”</i><br /><br />Fast forward to 1947 and the subcontinent, where Hiroko lands into another area full of upheaval. Going to Delhi, to the home of Konrad’s sister Ilse, now the married Elizabeth Burton, <b>Hiroko is trying to escape the brand of Hibakusha,</b> as hard a task as removing the bird shaped scars on her back from where the kimono she was wearing at the time of the bomb burned into her skin. Tensions in the area are chaotic with the upcoming partition into India and Pakistan, and it is here that we stumble into the world of the Burtons, a British middle aged couple, and Sajjad Ashraf, the husband James’s bored servant. It is here that, even as the outside world changes, it will be Hiroko’s presence that will cause the greatest change.<br /><br /><i>She saw her words filtering into his thoughts and becoming part of the way he saw the world.</i><br /><br />Japanese, British, Hindustani. In the first half of the book, Kamila Shamsie is obsessed with nationality:<b> which country do we come from, and how does it affect us?</b> The Burtons, with their disintegrating marriage and their son sent out of the country because of the conflict, are as removed a part of the subcontinent as Sajjad Ashraf is in love with it. His Dilli/Dehli, where his whole family resides, is his life. He is in love with the culture, the poetry, and as reluctant to leave the city, but his blooming romance with Hiroko and the subsequent events put a twist in fate that brings us, once again, down to the level of the human reacting to the large, the international, the encompassing. <br /><br /><i>It seemed to Sajjad these were the kinds of things said so that repetition made fact of conjecture. He’d know what to do with an Urdu masterpiece written by an Englishman. He’d read it. Why pretend it was more complicated than that?</i><br /><br />We move swiftly from the streets of Dilli to a pit stop in Istanbul before suddenly we’re in Karachi, the living, breathing city of Pakistan, where Shamsie’s prose shines at her best. It could be because she grew up here, but there is yearning in the writing here. If not for anything else, <b>books like these should be read just for the breathtaking command over words,</b> the mixing of emotion and thought and politics and culture all in one perfectly formed sentence.<br /><br /><i>He cursed under his breath the government which kept trying to force religion into everything public. His mother, with her most intimate relationship with Allah, would have personally knocked on the door of Army House and told the President he should have more shame than to ask all the citizens to conduct their love affairs with the Almighty out in the open.</i><br /><br />In Karachi is where the story starts to show its desire to connect its characters. <b>Raza Konrad Ashraf</b>, whose very name is an amalgamation of religions and nationalities, is the book’s product of interconnectedness, Hiroko and Sajjad’s son, and <b>Shamsie explores parenthood and adolescence like she understands what both these dual experiences can feel like. </b><br /><br /><i>She was overwhelmed by a felling of sorrow for her boy, for that look in her eyes which told her he knew and had always known that he would have to take that most exceptional part of himself and put it to one side. She knew what Sajjad would say if she tried to discuss it with him: ‘If the greatest loss of his life is the loss of a dream he’s always known to be a dream, then he’s among the fortunate ones.’ He’d be right, of course, but that didn’t stop this pulling at her heart.</i><br /><br />Throughout the book’s course we careen throughout history, from Nagasaki to Hindustan to Karachi and then finally, inevitably, to 2001, where the shadow of 9/11 looms large over New York City and Afghanistan. It is over here that the Weiss and the Burtons will find their way into the lives of the Tanaka and Ashrafs, all the while spanning across greater demographics. We are no longer just concerned with nationalities, but with ethnicities, with religion, with <b>the divergence of groups within groups and individuals whose particular choices can affect all those around them.</b><br /><br /><i>It seemed the most extraordinary privilege – to have forewarning of a swerve in history, to prepare for how your life would curve around that bend.</i><br /> <br />There is much to be said about the book’s ending, and about how who we are defines what we think. But speaking too openly will only reveal an ending that should be read to be savoured. <br /><br /><b>The recommendation:</b><br /><br />Recommended for everyone who is a fan of historical fiction/ interested in geopolitical change/ looking for a serious, good read. This book is literary fiction at its finest, not too pretentious or long to bore the reader, and not too glib or arrogant to put one off. Put this on your to-read list right now. <br /><br />**<br /><br />I review Pakistani Fiction, and talk about Pakistani fiction, and want to talk to people who like to talk about fiction (Pakistani and otherwise, take your pick.) To read more reviews or just contact me so you can talk about books, check out my <a href="http://thedesiwritingguide.blogspot.com/" rel="nofollow noopener">Blog</a> or follow me on <a href="https://twitter.com/AnumShaharyar" rel="nofollow noopener">Twitter</a>!</span>
  <a data-text-id="851931862918342968" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

            Shelves:

<span id="freeTextContainer6541154278565916689"><img src='https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1438900335i/15768871._SY540_.jpg' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/><br/><b>The Urakami Cathedral in Nagasaki, which was obliterated on 9 August 1945. The replacement was built in 1959 - Photograph: Shigeo Hayashi/Nagasaki Atomic Bomb Museum</b><br/><br/><b>”Sombras Queimadas”</b> o quinto romance da paquistanesa <b>Kamila Shamsie</b> (n. 1973) está dividido em quatro partes: <b>”O Mundo ainda Desconhecido” (Nagasáqui, 9 de Agosto de 1945)</b>, <b>”Pássaros Velados” (Deli, 1947)</b>, <b>”Guerreiros Meio-Anjos” (Paquistão, 1982 – 1983)</b> e <b>”A Velocidade Necessária para Substituir a Perda (Nova Iorque, Afeganistão, 20</b></span>
  <span id="freeText6541154278565916689" style="display:none"><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1438900335i/15768871._SY540_.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><b>The Urakami Cathedral in Nagasaki, which was obliterated on 9 August 1945. The replacement was built in 1959 - Photograph: Shigeo Hayashi/Nagasaki Atomic Bomb Museum</b><br><br><b>”Sombras Queimadas”</b> o quinto romance da paquistanesa <b>Kamila Shamsie</b> (n. 1973) está dividido em quatro partes: <b>”O Mundo ainda Desconhecido” (Nagasáqui, 9 de Agosto de 1945)</b>, <b>”Pássaros Velados” (Deli, 1947)</b>, <b>”Guerreiros Meio-Anjos” (Paquistão, 1982 – 1983)</b> e <b>”A Velocidade Necessária para Substituir a Perda (Nova Iorque, Afeganistão, 2001 – 2002)</b> numa narrativa que começa com um acontecimento trágico para a jovem mulher de vinte e um anos, Hiroko Tanaka, apaixonada pelo idealista alemão Konrad Weiss - o deflagrar a 9 de Agosto de 1945 da bomba atómica em Nagasáqui. <br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1438551428i/15727032._SX540_.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><b>Nagasáqui, Japão 9 de Agosto de 1945</b><br><br>Hiroko Tanaka acaba por sobreviver com queimaduras nas costas em forma de três pássaros, três grous pretos, modulados pelo estampado do quimono que vestia, cicatrizes para a vida e que relembram a morte do seu noivo Konrad Weiss, um amor e um futuro perdido, uma “sombra queimada”. <br>Em 1947, após um luto solitário e doloroso, Hiroko Tanaka parte para Deli, Índia em “busca” das raízes de Konrad Weiss, da meia-irmã, Elizabeth/Ilse, casada com o advogado colonialista inglês James Burton, que tem no jovem muçulmano Sajjad Ashraf, um “servo” e um empregado bonito, inteligente e bem-humorado.<br>Uma amizade e uma história amor que se inicia com consequências imprevisíveis… <br><b>”Sombras Queimadas”</b> é um livro complexo e abrangente, ambicioso na temática e nas ligações históricas entre os vários eventos, com um conjunto inesquecível de personagens, com destaque para Hiroko Tanaka e Sajjad Ashraf, o filho ambos Raza; os Burton, James e Elizabeth/Ilse, do filho do casal Henry/Harry e da sua filha Kim; um relato intenso e emocional sobre <b>“Duas famílias, duas versões da dança da aranha. Os Ashraf – Tanaka, os Weiss – Burton – as suas histórias juntas, a história de uma bomba, a história de uma pátria perdida, a história de um homem abatido a tiro junto às docas, a história do colete à prova de bala ignorado, a história de fugir sozinho do maior poder do Mundo.”</b> (Pág. 426)<br><b>Kamila Shamsie</b> escreve um excelente romance - numa narrativa intensa e uma prosa poética - fragmentado no tempo e no espaço, com a acção a decorrer em aproximadamente cinquenta e sete anos, com destaque para eventos históricos, alguns deles catastróficos – a deflagração da segunda bomba atómica em Nagasáqui, Japão, a “partilha” da Índia britânica, com a criação da Índia e do Paquistão, a construção da bomba atómica e os testes nucleares da Índia e do Paquistão, a invasão soviética do Afeganistão, o 11 de Setembro, a radicalização islâmica – num contexto de histórias pessoais e políticas, um relato convincente sobre o amor, a lealdade, a incompreensão, a desilusão, a desconfiança, a culpa, a traição e a redenção. <br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1438551428i/15727033._SX540_.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><b>Kamila Shamsie</b> (n. 1973)<br><br></span>

<div id="review_72785948" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/72785948" />
    <a title="Ruby" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/821493-ruby"><img alt="Ruby" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1201120519p2/821493.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/72785948?book_show_action=true">Sep 28, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ruby" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ruby" href="/user/show/821493-ruby">Ruby</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer72785948" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11427905458081980947">I read so much, and so quickly, that it takes a rare exception of a book to send me searching for post-it notes and a pen to write down quotes from the writing. I found the writing in this book so compelling, that I stopped reading everything else for two whole days and just immersed myself in the story of a woman who finds herself in the midst of several acts of war in the lifetime. From Nagasaki where she is scared mentally and physically, to Delhi where she runs with her new husband from the </span>
  <span id="freeText11427905458081980947" style="display:none">I read so much, and so quickly, that it takes a rare exception of a book to send me searching for post-it notes and a pen to write down quotes from the writing. I found the writing in this book so compelling, that I stopped reading everything else for two whole days and just immersed myself in the story of a woman who finds herself in the midst of several acts of war in the lifetime. From Nagasaki where she is scared mentally and physically, to Delhi where she runs with her new husband from the violence that surrounds the retreat of England, to the rise of the Taliban in Pakistan where she almost looses her son, to her retirement in New York city in 2001, where she lives with a dear old friend. There is so much that happens in this book, I really can't even begin to summarize the plot further than that.<br /><br />"Two years after the war, they could accept an ally of Hitler sooner than they could accept someone of a different class."<br /><br />Although all those circumstances may seem contrived to put together in one book, this book was so believable, that we kept catching ourselves at my bookclub talking about the characters as if they were people we knew. But in some ways they are. We are all expats living in Asia, most of us have lived in several countries, and most of us had had cross-cultural relationships. Although we haven't directly lived in the path of war, this book is us.<br /><br />"We were just young and foolish, what did we know about each other? Almost nothing. It was luck, pure luck, that we discovered after marriage that our natures were so sympathetic to each other."<br /><br />I don't know if a truer statement has been written about the vibe of an Asian city, "...world's urban tribes as they enter unfamiliar landscapes of chaos and possibility.. cars using their horns in a complicated and unrelenting exchange about power, intention, and mis-trust."</span>
  <a data-text-id="11427905458081980947" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

              <span id="reviewTextContainer62146719" class="readable"

<span id="freeTextContainer15999342256522875244">A wonderful read, terrifying in parts, heart-breaking in others and beautifully written throughout - this story has had a huge impact on me and I know it will linger for a long while.<br/><br/>From the beginning of the book when the atomic bomb is dropped on Nagasaki, the effects of this act and of war on the familes involved in the story line shows just how pointless war is.<br/><br/>Kamila Shamsie paints some vivid pictures that are difficult to shift and often invoke high emotion.<br/><br/>I'd highly recommend this novel</span>

          </span>

<div id="review_3015794413" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3015794413" />
    <a title="Tamara Agha-Jaffar" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/51003559-tamara-agha-jaffar"><img alt="Tamara Agha-Jaffar" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1452453836p2/51003559.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3015794413?book_show_action=true">Sep 25, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Tamara Agha-Jaffar" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Tamara Agha-Jaffar" href="/user/show/51003559-tamara-agha-jaffar">Tamara Agha-Jaffar</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/51003559-tamara-agha-jaffar?shelf=2020-mena-challenge">2020-mena-challenge</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/51003559-tamara-agha-jaffar?shelf=books-i-ve-reviewed">books-i-ve-reviewed</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/51003559-tamara-agha-jaffar?shelf=women-of-color">women-of-color</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/51003559-tamara-agha-jaffar?shelf=2020-read-women">2020-read-women</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/51003559-tamara-agha-jaffar?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/51003559-tamara-agha-jaffar?shelf=historical-fiction">historical-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/51003559-tamara-agha-jaffar?shelf=2020-reading-challenge">2020-reading-challenge</a>
          </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3015794413" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13953357949009551601">It is a saga of two families whose lives are inextricably intertwined. It is an epic tale sweeping across continents over a sixty-year period. <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/4101648.Burnt_Shadows' title='Burnt Shadows by Kamila Shamsie' rel='nofollow noopener'>Burnt Shadows</a> by <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/168076.Kamila_Shamsie' title='Kamila Shamsie' rel='nofollow noopener'>Kamila Shamsie</a> begins in Nagasaki on August 9, 1945; goes to India just before partition in 1947; to Pakistan in 1982-1983; and concludes in New York and Afghanistan in 2001-2002. <br/><br/>The central figure is Hiroko Tanaka, a Nagasaki resident. The novel opens with her as a twenty-one-year-old and engaged to a man of English and German descent. </span>
  <span id="freeText13953357949009551601" style="display:none">It is a saga of two families whose lives are inextricably intertwined. It is an epic tale sweeping across continents over a sixty-year period. <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/4101648.Burnt_Shadows" title="Burnt Shadows by Kamila Shamsie" rel="nofollow noopener">Burnt Shadows</a> by <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/168076.Kamila_Shamsie" title="Kamila Shamsie" rel="nofollow noopener">Kamila Shamsie</a> begins in Nagasaki on August 9, 1945; goes to India just before partition in 1947; to Pakistan in 1982-1983; and concludes in New York and Afghanistan in 2001-2002. <br /><br />The central figure is Hiroko Tanaka, a Nagasaki resident. The novel opens with her as a twenty-one-year-old and engaged to a man of English and German descent. When the atomic bomb is dropped on Nagasaki, Hiroko loses her fiancé and her father. In addition to her emotional and psychological scars, Hiroko carries disfiguring burn scars on her back. Having lost everything in Nagasaki, she decides to make a clean break. She visits her fiancé’s half-sister, Elizabeth Weiss, who lives in India with her husband, James Burton. There she meets and marries Sajjad Ashraf, a Muslim employed by the Burtons. The partition in India forces the newly-weds to relocate temporarily to Istanbul then to Pakistan which they call home for nearly twenty years until Sajjad’s untimely death. Hiroko then relocates to New York to live with Elizabeth. The ties connecting the Tanakas, the Ashrafs, and the Burtons extend to their respective children, Henry Burton and Raza Ashraf, and to Henry’s daughter, Kim Burton.<br /><br />Shamsie skillfully infuses the different time frames and locations with historical and political events. Through Hiroko’s eyes, we see Nagasaki in the aftermath of the atomic bomb. We see the escalating tensions in India between British colonists, Hindus, and Muslims. From neighboring Pakistan, we see Afghanistan’s armed struggle against Russian occupation. And in New York, we see the aftermath of 9/11. <br /><br />Set against the backdrop of global conflicts over a period of nearly sixty years, this family saga wrestles with a number of complex issues. Scenes throughout reveal cross-cultural conflicts, racism, cultural understanding, cultural arrogance, ethnocentrism, loyalty, sacrifice, family, othering, betrayal, and the displacement of a civilian population. The four sections are seamlessly woven together with transitional passages to explain the leaps in time and location. The characters are unique, believable, and speak in authentic voices. <br /><br />Shamsie’s portrayal of Hiroko is particularly effective. She emerges as independent, loving, strong, tender, adaptable, and the anchor which binds the narrative and families together. Her fluency in several languages illustrates how language facilitates understanding and appreciation of a culture. But fluency in languages and dialects can also have catastrophic consequences, as evidenced by Raza. Having inherited his mother’s language skills, Raza sets in motion a series of events which end tragically. Hiroko’s character also contrasts racism with acceptance of the other. When Kim Burton seeks understanding for reporting an Afghan Muslim to authorities solely on the basis of a shared religion with the 9/11 terrorists, Hiroko responds with, “Should I look at you and see Harry Truman?” Enough said.<br /><br />A very powerful and compelling novel showing the impact of global conflicts on the lives of individuals. Told with compassion and sensitivity in immersive, riveting language.<br /><br />Very highly recommended.<br /><br />My book reviews are also available at <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://www.tamaraaghajaffar.com">www.tamaraaghajaffar.com</a><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="13953357949009551601" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/61315671" />
    <a title="Anne" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2464996-anne"><img alt="Anne" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1246138510p2/2464996.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/61315671?book_show_action=true">Jun 27, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Anne" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Anne" href="/user/show/2464996-anne">Anne</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer61315671" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4557319808707525625">This book, from the Orange Prize shortlist, has had terribly mixed reviews. How can a book that tries to tie together the bombing of Nagasaki, the partition of India, the Afghan conflict and 9/11 possibly work? Well, it does - I absolutely loved it. Hiroko is a wonderful character - she lives on the page in a way a character hasn't for me in ages, and she's the anchor that holds this enormous story together. The writing is quite beautiful - some of the imagery will really stay with me, but it re</span>
  <span id="freeText4557319808707525625" style="display:none">This book, from the Orange Prize shortlist, has had terribly mixed reviews. How can a book that tries to tie together the bombing of Nagasaki, the partition of India, the Afghan conflict and 9/11 possibly work? Well, it does - I absolutely loved it. Hiroko is a wonderful character - she lives on the page in a way a character hasn't for me in ages, and she's the anchor that holds this enormous story together. The writing is quite beautiful - some of the imagery will really stay with me, but it really doesn't spoil the narative, quite the opposite. The moments of tenderness - her time with Konrad, the beginning of her relationship with her husband, after some of the deaths (there's a few - they make you ache with grief...) - are quite superb. The narrative thrust is sustained and ambitious - it's a blooming good story, well told. Learnt a fair bit about the politics (India/Pakistan/Afghanistan) that I didn't know before too, but in the nicest way - by slow immersion in a wonderful story. A definite 10/10 for me... <br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="4557319808707525625" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_188519263" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/188519263" />
    <a title="Sana" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5862550-sana"><img alt="Sana" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1557438942p2/5862550.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/188519263?book_show_action=true">Jul 24, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Sana" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Sana" href="/user/show/5862550-sana">Sana</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5862550-sana?shelf=read-in-2011">read-in-2011</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5862550-sana?shelf=these-family-dynamics">these-family-dynamics</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer188519263" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2325999397582271445">The journey from Hiroko Tanaka to an almost Hiroko Konrad and finally, Hiroko Ashraf was intensely poetic and linked to the many absurdities of life. Everything written in the book can be reflected in one simple phrase, "The speed necessary to replace loss." More than a search for identity, Burnt Shadows is a tale about learning the secret about loss. There is no overcoming, just a bitter fading of it and an ever pronounceable taste that can surface anytime.<br/><br/>For Raza Konrad Ashraf, the narration </span>
  <span id="freeText2325999397582271445" style="display:none">The journey from Hiroko Tanaka to an almost Hiroko Konrad and finally, Hiroko Ashraf was intensely poetic and linked to the many absurdities of life. Everything written in the book can be reflected in one simple phrase, "The speed necessary to replace loss." More than a search for identity, Burnt Shadows is a tale about learning the secret about loss. There is no overcoming, just a bitter fading of it and an ever pronounceable taste that can surface anytime.<br /><br />For Raza Konrad Ashraf, the narration had a touch of belonging and for him, life was but a series of throwing caution to the wind. He found refuge in being Raza Hazara and that was when he felt as he belonged the most. He chose to be a deserter, not by willingness, but it was time that stretched him far away from the kind of life he always dreamed about. His character teaches a lot about being a child of beautiful parents. Moreover, his ability to converse in many languages makes him all too unforgettable.<br /><br />For Sajjad Ashraf, his choices reflected the person that he aspired to be and never realized that it didn't really matter to people close to him. His Dilli and his feelings of always being underestimated weren't lost on me.<br /><br />Kamila Shamsie has woven a world, an alternate universe really, that shows that a bomb not only spreads hatred, it destroys the aspirations that come attached with life. I found Burnt Shadows to be an amalgamation of characters connected through a dead link that was Konrad Weiss. From Jack to Elizabeth, Raza to Harry and finally Hiroko to Ilse and Kim. The loyalties tested at every step of the way and nativeness being the determining factor between friends. From Burtons to Ashrafs and back to the Burtons; time doesn't stop for anyone long enough to take them with it.</span>
  <a data-text-id="2325999397582271445" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>
