    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_133712342" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/133712342" />
    <a title="okyrhoe" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/825356-okyrhoe"><img alt="okyrhoe" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1373221413p2/825356.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/133712342?book_show_action=true">Dec 05, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="okyrhoe" class="user" itemprop="url" name="okyrhoe" href="/user/show/825356-okyrhoe">okyrhoe</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781408807019" href="/book/show/9836040-burnt-shadows">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/825356-okyrhoe?shelf=bookcrossing">bookcrossing</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/825356-okyrhoe?shelf=u-s-a">u-s-a</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/825356-okyrhoe?shelf=books-i-own">books-i-own</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/825356-okyrhoe?shelf=pakistan">pakistan</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/825356-okyrhoe?shelf=india">india</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/825356-okyrhoe?shelf=change-cover">change-cover</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer133712342" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6471525706635381953">I actually enjoyed reading it, and I would recommend it to others. But it failed to 'wow' me.... <br/>For some reason the plot seemed a tad too obvious. I could tell more or less what was going to happen, in which direction the scenes were developing. So I was reading at two levels, one to read the plot, and another to sense/study the words on the page. That second aspect however wasn't taking me anywhere. I expected to be engaged by the narrative exposition – looking forward to discover irony, doubl</span>
  <span id="freeText6471525706635381953" style="display:none">I actually enjoyed reading it, and I would recommend it to others. But it failed to 'wow' me.... <br />For some reason the plot seemed a tad too obvious. I could tell more or less what was going to happen, in which direction the scenes were developing. So I was reading at two levels, one to read the plot, and another to sense/study the words on the page. That second aspect however wasn't taking me anywhere. I expected to be engaged by the narrative exposition – looking forward to discover irony, double meanings, etc. - the kind of elements I had previously admired in Shamsie's novel Broken Verses. Somehow the story and the telling of it felt as if the novel lacked much of that. <br />I had the distinct impression the novel was written with a film adaptation in mind. “The English Patient” I thought on a number of times. Eventually I came to the final section’s title; it validated my impression. <br />Without saying it's necessarily a bad thing, there were also moments when works by other writers came to mind (Nicole Krauss <i>The History of Love</i>, Stephanie Kallos <i>Broken for You</i>, Marina Lewicka <i>We Are All Made of Glue</i>, Andre Dubus III <i>The House of Sand and Fog</i>). I'm not saying that Shamsie is emulating any of these works, it's just that her novel didn't seem distinctively unique from these other works with similar themes. <br />If <i>Burnt Shadows</i> is derivative of any particular story, Vikram Seth's <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/860388.Two_Lives" rel="nofollow noopener">Two Lives</a> (a biography of his Indian uncle who married a German woman in England after WWII) may have been a major source: Hiroko's stoic quietude is an apt homage to V. Seth's German "Aunty." <br />And for Raza Hazara's character, I would venture to guess the <a href="http://www.jezail.org/02_dreamsmith2001/35AF_MAV.html" rel="nofollow noopener">tragic</a>&amp; <a href="http://circleof13.blogspot.com/2008/05/tribute-to-carlos-mavroleon.html" rel="nofollow noopener">complex</a> story of <a href="http://www.youtube.com/watch?v=tl2XyN-hfNI" rel="nofollow noopener">Carlos Mavroleon</a> may have been an inspiration. <br /><br />The story should have <a href="http://www.youtube.com/watch?v=_EOeRb-ddqc" rel="nofollow noopener">gotten under my skin</a>, but it didn’t. Why not? It's not that I sensed the plot elements lacked credibility, or that these characters' coming together, drawing apart, only to be united again, seemed too farfetched to bear a resemblance to reality. Far from it, it mirrored my own family’s photo album (WWII, post-Partition Karachi, the Suez, a cemetary in Korea, etc.) and I had no problem with all these seemingly disparate elements coming together in a six-degrees of separation kind of way. <br />Nearing the final page of the book, I tried to find the root of my lack of excitement over this novel. One thing that bothered me is the “young” generation's (Kim's and Raza's) lack of a coherent political conscience. At this point I felt the characters lacked a substantial dimension; they became pawns in the storyboard the author was setting up for the final climactic scene. Despite the complexity of their heritage and their "inside" knowledge of history's details, they come off as somewhat naive (in an insular sort of way, which they clearly weren't) and that did not convince me, given their background. More than anything, the last 40-50 pages of the book (the New York section) are the most "screenplayish" of all. It's all about plot; there is nothing of the carefully crafted dialogue and scenic descriptions of the first part of the book (the Nagasaki and Dilli sections).<br />Another thing I felt cheated by is that we are told time and time again that Hiroko and Raza are fascinated by language, yet that is not reflected into the work itself. Having read Shamsie's <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/290389.Broken_Verses" rel="nofollow noopener">Broken Verses</a>, where the literary element is an integral feature of the story, I expected to find that kind of meta-fictional play at work here, too, but it is missing. </span>
  <a data-text-id="6471525706635381953" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_74257525" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/74257525" />
    <a title="Beverly" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/876421-beverly"><img alt="Beverly" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1386630133p2/876421.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/74257525?book_show_action=true">Oct 12, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Beverly" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Beverly" href="/user/show/876421-beverly">Beverly</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/876421-beverly?shelf=historical-fiction">historical-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/876421-beverly?shelf=asia">asia</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer74257525" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4194049314347955298">Burnt Shadows by Kamila Shamsie is an ambitious epic book that grabs you in the prologue, as an unnamed narrator is disrobed and left to wait naked with only a steel bench to sit on. His thought is – “How did it come to this.” How stark is this setting – but the grace of the language warns you that this is a story that you want to see unfold.<br/><br/>The story spans 60 years and takes the reader to five different countries: Nagasaki, August 1945; Delhi 1947; Pakistan 1982-3; and New York/ Afghanistan 200</span>
  <span id="freeText4194049314347955298" style="display:none">Burnt Shadows by Kamila Shamsie is an ambitious epic book that grabs you in the prologue, as an unnamed narrator is disrobed and left to wait naked with only a steel bench to sit on. His thought is – “How did it come to this.” How stark is this setting – but the grace of the language warns you that this is a story that you want to see unfold.<br /><br />The story spans 60 years and takes the reader to five different countries: Nagasaki, August 1945; Delhi 1947; Pakistan 1982-3; and New York/ Afghanistan 2001-2; and the connecting points for two families whose family members will have intimate knowledge of the destruction of war. It all starts in the morning of August 9, 1945 before the bomb was dropped on Nagasaki and we are introduced to schoolteacher, Hiroko Tanaka and a man from Berlin, Konrad Weiss. Both are looking forward to the end of the war so that life gets back to normal and they can be wed. But history has other plans, and Hiroko whose language skills has her working for the Americans during the occupation. Unable to work closely with the “terrorists” who have invaded her country, she flees to Delphi to Konrad’s sister. Hiroko is the one character that is present throughout the book and helps thread the book themes together.<br /><br />This is an elegantly written story that allows the reader to understand how history affects our relationships with each other, Sometimes history defers relationships and others relationships survive despite the history. In each of the major parts of the book – there are historical events that are well known but what is not known is how it affects individuals who only want “to farm their land and raise their families.” There are themes of sameness and otherness in different cultures and the issues that one can have when trying to be the same. This book shows how a terrorist is defined is dependent on whose face you are looking at based on your own individual history. <br /><br />I recommend this book to fans of historical fiction and world events. Readers of literary fiction will enjoy this poetic story with the universal themes of humanity and characters finding a way to bring satisfaction to their individual lives.<br /><br />Reviewed by Beverly<br />APOOO BookClub<br />May 6, 2009<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="4194049314347955298" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

  <span id="freeText2117643543463695850" style="display:none">Undoubtedly a 5.0. <br /><br />How swiftly the author has swept through the eras; three generations suffering at the hand of fate.. and war. How effortlessly she has woven the myriad of ideas, linking them with each other through real life occurrences is particularly commendable. <br /><br />Not only is this novel thought provoking but it also tells a tale of recent times. How war is bringing nothing but turmoil and destruction. How misunderstandings and lack of communication gives chance for evil to prosper and one to end up living a life of regrets. How the need for power has surpassed something as basic and vital as humanity. <br /><br />The details &amp; description of events of Nagasaki through partition of Indo-Pak to occurrence and impact of 9/11 were reflected &amp; narrated beautifully. <br /><br />It was an exceptional read. I enjoyed every single moment of it.</span>

<div id="review_50267824" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/50267824" />
    <a title="Chrissie" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/632247-chrissie"><img alt="Chrissie" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1321799585p2/632247.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/50267824?book_show_action=true">Mar 24, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer50267824" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11528567389643688227">NO SPOILERS<br/><br/>I finished this last night. Three or four stars? Do I REALLY like it or do I like it. While I was reading it, I REALLY liked it, but with time it is the story that will remain not all the wonderful lines that are so intriguing. I think it will turn into an "I liked it" book. You will thoroughly enjoy the time spent with this book if you enjoyed the quotes below. Don't think three stars means, aacch choose something else. I loved it b/c it was thought provoking. The last third of the b</span>
  <span id="freeText11528567389643688227" style="display:none">NO SPOILERS<br /><br />I finished this last night. Three or four stars? Do I REALLY like it or do I like it. While I was reading it, I REALLY liked it, but with time it is the story that will remain not all the wonderful lines that are so intriguing. I think it will turn into an "I liked it" book. You will thoroughly enjoy the time spent with this book if you enjoyed the quotes below. Don't think three stars means, aacch choose something else. I loved it b/c it was thought provoking. The last third of the book goes off in a different tangent. It has a message that is loud and clear. It is not that a disagree with that message, just that I prefer thinking through these things on my own. The end was to blatant for me. I am usually left wondering a bit about what I think. Where do I stand? This author seems to KNOW exactly what she wanted to say. I THINK I do agree with her,...., but I would rather think and mull over an issure than be given a clear answer. So I am giving the book three stars. Sorry if I am unclear. It is the confidence in the author's own beliefs, her need to send a message, that put me off. She knows exactly where she stands. I don't and rarely ever do. Do read the book!<br /><br />Through page 256:I don't understand how you cannot like this book. Maybe you have to be a little older to have experienced all the different parts of life.<br /><br />Here follows a quote about family relationships:<br /><br />"They both knew what it was to be an easily erased entry in the cluttered schedule of a beloved relative." (page 255)<br /><br />Here follows a quote about aging:<br /><br />"I wish I were old, Kim thought, watching the two women. Really old. Old enough to have left everything troublesome behind - careers, lovers, regrets, Fathers, Mothers. Were you never old enough for that?" (page 253)<br /><br />Here follows a quote about your kids:<br /><br />"To often around her father, she couldn't stop being a teenager either in adulation or sulleness." (page 253)<br /><br />This book so well describes the difficulties involved in raising kids. Teen and separation difficulties. No matter what you do, your heart is torn, and yet at the same time you love and go on loving. <br /><br />And that long book description is not what this book is about. It is not a spoiler. It is the second to the last paragraph of the book description that is most relevant. <br /><br />hrough page 152: I loved chapter 11 – describing sex while the Burtons repeatedly were asking each other where the twoyoung lovers could possibly have gone.<br /><br />Through page 116 : I particularly like this book for two reasons The first is the strength with which the reader feels for the central characters. Each are very different, and yet you feel you understand each one’s fears and joys. It is the involvement with the characters’ emotional being that is outstanding. I feel I know these people. I feel I can understand why they make the choices they make. I would not necessarily make the same choices but I do understand their choices. More than understanding, I FEEL what has prompted these choices. This is particularly true of Hiroko and Sajjad. Through Hiroko, you learn about the bombing and its aftermath in Nagasaki . Through Elizabeth and James Burton, you learn about the English living in Delhi right before Partition, right before the formation of independent India and East and West Pakistan. Through Sajjad you learn about the people of the Muslim faith living in India before the creation of Pakistan, the conflicts between Sikhs and Hindus and Muslims. It has definitely been helpful to read<a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/825690.Indian_Summer_The_Secret_History_of_the_End_of_an_Empire" title="Indian Summer The Secret History of the End of an Empire by Alex von Tunzelmann" rel="nofollow noopener">Indian Summer: The Secret History of the End of an Empire</a>, before reading this novel. It helps to know the background of the Muslim League, Jinnah and all the others who helped create Pakistan and India. There are wonderful myths here that add to the historical facts presented in Indian Summer. Muslim reverence for spiders is but one example. <br /><br />The second strength of the book is the wonderful writing. It isn’t blatant., but instead thought provoking. I often stop and ask myself: could the author be saying this?! I like the ambiguity. I stop and question what I feel about a given topic. In addition there is humor:<br /><br /> (The memory of) the road leading to the street-car, where she’d responded to his gloomy complaints about rationing (with) ‘Yes, We Have No Bananas’ …” (page 17)<br /><br />I started singing when I read that line! Growing love is beautifully described through a little jumping fish:<br /><br />“Megane-Bashi, or Spectacles Bridge, where they had been standing, looking into the water when a small silver fish leapt out of Konrad’s reflected chest and dived into her reflection and she said, ‘Oh,’ and stepped back, almost loosing her balance, so he had to put his arm around her waist to steady her.” (page17)<br /><br />Well I love the metaphor! The author mentions here “Spectacles Bridge”, and the reader wonders about the peculiar name.. Later this bridge name IS explained, and it is all the more wonderful b/c you had to wait for the explanation! Read the book, and you will smile too when you draw an image of this bridge in your mind.<br /><br />What else? I love the philosophical questions. After the horrors of WW2 and the bombing in Japan and the riots and killings in India :<br /><br />“…if these days teach us anything, it’s that all we can do in preparation for tomorrow is nothing. So let’s talk about today…..” (page 115)<br /><br />Concerning the bombing in Nagasaki: <br /><br />“…I still don’t understand. Why did they have to do it? Why a second bomb? Even the first is beyond anything I can… but a second? You do that, and see what you’ve done, and then you do it again. How is that…..?” (page 99)<br /><br />There are so many sentences I could quote. So many interesting thoughts – how people like constancy, how it feels to settle and never really fit into a new country, the ties of family. I could go on and on.<br /><br />This book is so much more than how the plot develops from point A to Z…… <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="11528567389643688227" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<tr class="no_border feedFooterReview" id="update_comment_stuff_Review50267824">

<div id="review_64290322" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/64290322" />
    <a title="Lanew-yorkaise" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2543425-lanew-yorkaise"><img alt="Lanew-yorkaise" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/u_50x66-632230dc9882b4352d753eedf9396530.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/64290322?book_show_action=true">Jul 20, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Lanew-yorkaise" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Lanew-yorkaise" href="/user/show/2543425-lanew-yorkaise">Lanew-yorkaise</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer64290322" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5115860390709087486">From <a target='_blank' rel='noopener nofollow' href='http://lanew-yorkaise.com/'>http://lanew-yorkaise.com/</a> <br/><br/>Maybe it’s because I spent a good part of my college years studying trauma and how people experience and record it; maybe because World War II and its fallout—both figurative and literal—is a topic I find myself drawn to again and again (my thesis was based on an oral history project I conducted that recorded the stories of college students-turned-soldiers in the ‘40s.) Maybe it’s because the writing is so damn lush, the characters so real. Whatever the reason, Ka</span>
  <span id="freeText5115860390709087486" style="display:none">From <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://lanew-yorkaise.com/">http://lanew-yorkaise.com/</a> <br /><br />Maybe it’s because I spent a good part of my college years studying trauma and how people experience and record it; maybe because World War II and its fallout—both figurative and literal—is a topic I find myself drawn to again and again (my thesis was based on an oral history project I conducted that recorded the stories of college students-turned-soldiers in the ‘40s.) Maybe it’s because the writing is so damn lush, the characters so real. Whatever the reason, Kamila Shamsie’s Burnt Shadows struck a chord with me, and my mind is still wrapping itself around the vibrations.<br /><br /> Burnt Shadows opens on August 9, 1945 in Nagasaki, Japan, where 21-year-old Hiroko Tanaka is a young translator in love with a man. Not just any man: Konrad Weiss is a foreigner, and a German at that—doubly taboo.<br /><br /> The moment Hiroko steps out onto the veranda in a kimono embroidered with two cranes in flight is a moment seared into her memory, just as the shape of those cranes was to become seared into her flesh. In the time it took for the atomic bomb to flash its blinding light over Nagasaki, Hiroko’s entire world shifted.<br /><br />Shamsie’s depiction of the blazing fires and confusion in those horrific moments is rendered in stark contrast to the brief glimpse of dappled sunlight and budding love of earlier scenes, in an August heat that seems as if it could as easily be in Central Park as it could be in a city called Nagasaki.<br /><br /> Two years later, Hiroko leaves the city and its ashes behind to embark on a trip to Delhi, where the imminence of the separation of India from mother England is palpably pressing upon Konrad’s half-sister, Elizabeth, and her English husband, James Burton. The Burtons uneasily coexist with Konrad’s friend Sajjad Ashraf, an Indian man apprenticed to the Englishman to study law. Hiroko enters this place of blurred hierarchies and crumbling traditions as an outsider, not quite white but close enough. We see Delhi on the verge of partition through the eyes of this curious Japanese woman in men’s trousers.<br /><br />It is with Hiroko’s gaze that we see the lush gardens of the officers’ wives, through her ears that we overhear the marital brawls between a domineering husband about to lose the source of his power and a lonely wife, about to loose the place she has made a home. And always, the feeling of the burns on our backs, coloring the edges of our vision and affecting all we see.<br /><br />India’s partition and the creation of Pakistan form the third shift of worlds that thrusts Hiroko’s life into disarray. Ever the survivor, we follow Hiroko to a new life in Pakistan where she raises a son caught between two cultures, his mother’s legacy a knack for languages, the tools of a world babbling away in many tongues.<br /><br />From an erupting Pakistan to a New York City still reeling from the attacks of 9/11 to war-torn Afghanistan, Shamsie shows us the now-old woman still haunted by the ties that bind Ashraf, Burton and Tanaka through decades of different countries in upheaval; the seismic shifts of our century.<br /><br />The genius of Shamsie’s work is to recreate the sound and the fury of these outwardly disparate worlds, making pivotal historical events more real through the tool of the multi-generational novel. We see the young lovers in Nagasaki and the blinding light of the bomb; feel the confusion of the heat between Hiroko and another outsider in the orderly English garden in the midst of a disorderly Delhi. Getting eerily closer to our own time, we see the deserts of Pakistan from the back of a truck as young boys go off on a mission meant for grown men. And so close it burns: a family from a land of terrorists living in a New York City fighting terror, skyline still smoldering as it picks up the pieces of its vulnerability.<br /><br /> To someone who has not read the book, the fact that one woman could have been at all these places at exactly the right times to see such changes may seem absurd. Sometimes reality is stranger than fiction (think of the English couple that witnessed three terrorist attacks in three different countries.)<br /><br /> Burnt Shadows is vast in scope and meticulously researched; each era that the author presents thoroughly enfolds the reader with the magic of Shamsie’s storytelling.<br /><br />Above all, the book is human: Shamsie shows us how the dueling powers of memory and nationality forge a new identity for a family set adrift in the fate of a twisted century.<br /><br />KAMILA SHAMSIE was born in 1973 in Karachi. She has studied and taught in the USA. Two of her previous novels, Kartography and Broken Verses, have won awards from Pakistan’s Academey of Letters. She writes for The Guardian (UK) and frequently broadcasts on the BBC. Burnt Shadows is a finalist for the Orange Prize<br /><br /><a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://lanew-yorkaise.com/?p=562">http://lanew-yorkaise.com/?p=562</a><br /><br />For more reviews of books with international themes or books in translation, please visit <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://www.LaNew-Yorkaise.com">www.LaNew-Yorkaise.com</a></span>
  <a data-text-id="5115860390709087486" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <span id="freeText586652977622810086" style="display:none">I started this book with no hope of ever liking it. Mainly because it had names in Japanese and German that i could not pronounce. It was slow in some places but picked the pace in the end. <br />It is precisely the story of a woman who leaves Japan in hope of a better world trying to out run her past, the story is 55 years of her life from there on.<br />It shows world at its worst and how love, friendship, forgiveness and loyalty can still exist in it. How every decision we make is not independent it concerns those who are in our in lives directly or indirectly. Words hurt, trust broken once never really is recovered. <br />We can find comfort and home in most unlikely places and among most unlikely people. <br />One of the reasons i liked it so much that it had a lot of history relating to our region. The writer did a lot of research, to make the events of the world sync in with story. The book was tragedy bound to happen. Did not make it any less interesting read. The writer depicts certain events with so much conviction that u feel the pain and desperation and helplessness of people. It is one of those book which reminds of all that could be but was not because cruelties of humans towards humans are much greater than any damage a natural disaster can cause. </span>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_60666807" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/60666807" />
    <a title="Heather" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/746698-heather"><img alt="Heather" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1232126089p2/746698.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/60666807?book_show_action=true">Jun 22, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Heather" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Heather" href="/user/show/746698-heather">Heather</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer60666807" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4739095447876670013">Hiroko Tanaka's life has been irrevocably marred by the American bombing of Nagasaki in the summer of 1945. Not only did she lose her father, village, and way of life, but also the young German artist Konrad, with whom she was beginning a relationship. After the kimono she was wearing in the blast becomes fused with her skin, she bears scars shaped like birds across her back. It is with these painful scars and memories that she leaves Japan, unable to find her place in society after the war. Hir</span>
  <span id="freeText4739095447876670013" style="display:none">Hiroko Tanaka's life has been irrevocably marred by the American bombing of Nagasaki in the summer of 1945. Not only did she lose her father, village, and way of life, but also the young German artist Konrad, with whom she was beginning a relationship. After the kimono she was wearing in the blast becomes fused with her skin, she bears scars shaped like birds across her back. It is with these painful scars and memories that she leaves Japan, unable to find her place in society after the war. Hiroko travels to New Delhi, where she meets Konrad's half-sister Ilse and her husband James, who agree to shelter her in honor of Konrad's memory. James employs an ersatz assistant, Sajjad, who is in reality only there to provide him amusement and company. When Hiroko and Sajjad begin to have feelings for each other, a set of events occurs that enmesh two families through the generations and take them across the ocean from India to Pakistan to America. Along the way they face the realities of the Partition of India, the nuclear threats of Pakistan, and the more modern struggle of terrorism in America. Fraught with the themes of cultural alienation and identity, Burnt Shadows gives an account of those whose lives are built despite and amidst the destruction of war.<br /><br />The story was definitely powerful and emotional, but there was a lot there to digest. At times I think that the author overreached in portraying this story, with too many elements in close quarters. I understand that this story was meant to capture a large span of time and encompass several important cultural themes and events, but at times it felt like there were too many balls in the air at once.<br /><br />The story and message in this book prompted me to put a human face to the complications that arise during war, not only governmental, but socially and personally. The moral and personal struggles of the characters as a whole were much more complex and at times more ambiguous than in any other novel I had read previously about war and it's impacts; at times, even my own opinions were muddy about certain aspects. Many of themes and conversations of this book revolved around the characters' feelings for their homeland, prior to and during war. Some things seemed very black and white while others were clothed in subtle shades of gray. In a way, the book shattered a lot of the preconceived notions that I had, not only about political hostilities, but about the effect they have on the future generations. When Hiroko and Sajeed's young son decides to get involved with a group of young Afghans who are training for guerrilla combat, it was hard for me to find an acceptable position to take. Was I to feel upset that the boy had flown into the arms of terrorism, or was I to understand that this was only a misguided attempt to find acceptance with a group of men who seemingly idolized him?<br /><br />Some of the things I found interesting were not only the larger issues of cultural identity and societal belonging, but the smaller and finer character motivations and interactions that filled the pages. I think it would be too simple to focus on these things though. The issues that the book presents are meant to be much more oblique and all-encompassing than that, so I will just say that the variegated relationships, tensions, and conversations that these characters were embroiled in were both impressive and authentic. They lent a depth to the story that made it more interesting. The characters in this book inspired reaction, and whether it was frustration, anger or sympathy, I found myself being much more involved with them then I have with other characters while reading similar books.<br /><br />The latter half of the book examines terrorism in a manner that I found compelling, and essentially gave several differing viewpoints of the issue from both the accused and the accuser. This was done in a manner I had not been expecting or familiar with and gave me even more to think about. The conclusion of the book left me stunned. I think that the author really did more to leave me embroiled and impassioned in the last few pages of the book then anywhere else. I wanted there to be a happy ending, but like life, the reality is that things sometimes end messily.<br /><br />As I have said before, this is a book that made me do some deep thinking. Its story, characterization, and ultimately, its conclusion were multi-faceted and elaborate and I appreciated both the scope and density of the story that was told. I think this book definitely had a statement to make, on many levels, and it succeeded in a way that went beyond the boundaries of earnest storytelling. It was both a plausible and affecting read, and one that I ended up enjoying. Aside from the cluttered manner of the plot, I think that readers of historical fiction would really enjoy the wide range in this book, and those that like books that are character driven would also get a lot out of it. </span>
  <a data-text-id="4739095447876670013" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

  <span id="freeText3627123988159359609" style="display:none">I am absolutely loving this book! Favorite quotes so far:<br /><br />(On debates regarding the formation of Pakistan) And so it went on and on, and in each group Sajjad found those who made complete sense and in each group also those whose opinions made him want to scatter seeds over the speakers so the pigeons would swoop down and stop their words with a tumult of feathers.<br /><br />(On the aftermath of the atomic bomb dropped on Nagasaki) ...he knew by her voice that he was going to hear something that she would speak of to almost no one else. "There was the one about the girl whose dying father slithers towards her in the shape of a lizard; she is so horrified by his grotesqueness it takes her years to understand that his final act was to come towards her, after a lifetime of walking away. The one about the boy shaken out of his life and told that was a dream, and so was everyone he loved in it - this charred world, this prison, this aloneness is reality. The one about the purple-backed bookcreatures with broken spines who immolate themselves rather than exist in a world in which everything written in them is shown to be fantasy. The woman who loses all feeling, fire entering from her back and searing her heart, so it's possible for her to see a baby's corpse and think only, There's another one. The men and women who walk through shadow-worlds in search of the ones they loved. Monsters who spread their wings and land on human skin, resting there, biding their time. The army of fire demons, dropped from the sky, who kill with an embrace. The schoolteacher in a world where textbooks come to life; she cannot escape from the anatomy text, its illustrations following her everywhere - bodies without skin, bodies with organs on display, bodies that reveal what happens to bodies when nothing in them works any more."<br /><br />...Hiroko was already bending over her husband and telling him to eat some toast.<br />"I'm dying," Sajjad said. "I'm dead already. I'm in hell."<br />"If this is hell why am I here?" Hiroko demanded, a hand on her hip.<br />Sajjad opened one eye.<br />"You've come to rescue me?" he said hopefully.<br />"Yes," she said. "With toast. Eat it and stop complaining, you silly drunkard."</span>

<span id="freeTextContainer12002673953699765844">Burnt Shadows (audio book)Burnt Shadows, by Pakistani author Kamila Shamsie, was nominated for the Orange Prize in 2009. Ambitious in its scope, it is a compelling novel that loses its way at the end.<br/><br/>Capably narrated in this audio book edition by Jane McDowell who only occasionally confuses the multiple accents she has to portray, Burnt Shadows is the story of Hiroko Tanaka, who survives the Nagasaki bomb blast but loses everything she holds dear. This novel vividly portrays the profound bewilde</span>
  <span id="freeText12002673953699765844" style="display:none">Burnt Shadows (audio book)Burnt Shadows, by Pakistani author Kamila Shamsie, was nominated for the Orange Prize in 2009. Ambitious in its scope, it is a compelling novel that loses its way at the end.<br /><br />Capably narrated in this audio book edition by Jane McDowell who only occasionally confuses the multiple accents she has to portray, Burnt Shadows is the story of Hiroko Tanaka, who survives the Nagasaki bomb blast but loses everything she holds dear. This novel vividly portrays the profound bewilderment that people must have felt when they emerged into the devastation and saw nothing of what had been there before. Ostracised by her own people as one of the Hibakusha (explosion-affected people) she moves to Delhi in pre-partition India to visit the family of Konrad Weiss, the German translator she had agreed to marry on the day of bomb blast. Although not really made welcome by James Burton, a British diplomat, Hiroko becomes a semi-permanent guest – and friend to Ilse, his discontented wife. She gets work as a translator, and despite her reserved nature, gradually forms an attachment to Sajjad Ashraf, who works for the Burtons, and teaches her Urdu.<br /><br />To read the rest of my review please visit <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://anzlitlovers.com/2013/06/02/burnt-shadows-by-kamila-shamsie-narrated-by-jane-mcdowell/">http://anzlitlovers.com/2013/06/02/bu...</a> but beware, there are spoilers there. </span>
  <a data-text-id="12002673953699765844" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<span id="freeTextContainer13181280996649903505">Just finished this book for book club. I've had so much on recently so it took me longer to get through than is ideal, but even so with all the breaks, this books really impactful. <br/><br/>Reading the blurb I wondered if it was setting itself up for a bit of a defeat; how could Shamsie possibly cover Nagasaki, partition of India, 9/11, and the Afghan wars in a single story?!?   <span id="freeText13181280996649903505" style="display:none">Just finished this book for book club. I've had so much on recently so it took me longer to get through than is ideal, but even so with all the breaks, this books really impactful. <br /><br />Reading the blurb I wondered if it was setting itself up for a bit of a defeat; how could Shamsie possibly cover Nagasaki, partition of India, 9/11, and the Afghan wars in a single story?!? But she does, and rather masterfully at that. It's one of those books I honestly think should be compulsory reading in schools, for all the perspectives and humanisation it offers in a world where dehumanising and othering is all too increasingly becoming a terrifying norm. </span>

<div id="review_1218964386" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1218964386" />
    <a title="Bharath" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/12800930-bharath"><img alt="Bharath" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1523678858p2/12800930.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1218964386?book_show_action=true">Mar 05, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Bharath" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Bharath" href="/user/show/12800930-bharath">Bharath</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1218964386" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14460422081166108555">Burnt Shadows is an unusual story. There are strong characters in Hiroko and Sajjad which make for great reading. The story comes across as somewhat contrived though, moving from Nagasaki to Delhi to Pakistan to New York. The motivation and behaviour of some of the characters; especially Raza is difficult to understand. The book moves well towards the end and finishes with some good sequences, examining attitudes and stereotypes, and tragedy which hides behind the so called larger good. For the </span>
  <span id="freeText14460422081166108555" style="display:none">Burnt Shadows is an unusual story. There are strong characters in Hiroko and Sajjad which make for great reading. The story comes across as somewhat contrived though, moving from Nagasaki to Delhi to Pakistan to New York. The motivation and behaviour of some of the characters; especially Raza is difficult to understand. The book moves well towards the end and finishes with some good sequences, examining attitudes and stereotypes, and tragedy which hides behind the so called larger good. For the mature and wise characters of Hiroko and Sajjad, this book is worth a read.</span>
  <a data-text-id="14460422081166108555" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

Although I musts ay, the dialogues at the end of the book were some of the very best ones! That could be the reason for the critical acclaim, the insight of the author related to the wars of nations!</span>

//<![CDATA[  

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('TdaqfyUfZKaD/FAz1KWppk/nP+H9piw/oVXMc1GZ9G53N6QQTcCK8MeqUigzXkyziYueTLTdJtQOIawkaFCRJA==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('KlSqjYv38ztEZekoPtBifwr1waBRPSZ1Fl9PPYjUZDUQtaTi4ygdbQAz6zPZK4dqzJlgDRhGLJ65Ky9qsR0Bfw==')})
  }
//]]>

      var newTip = new Tip($('bookCover_15798312'), "\n\n  <h2><a class=\"readable bookTitle\" href=\"https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/15798312-the-blind-man-s-garden?from_choice=false&amp;from_home_module=false\">The Blind Man&#39;s Garden<\/a><\/h2>\n\n      <div>\n        by <a class=\"authorName\" href=\"/author/show/24775.Nadeem_Aslam?a=9&amp;origin=related_works\">Nadeem Aslam<\/a>\n      <\/div>\n\n          <div class=\"smallText uitext darkGreyText\">\n            <span class=\"minirating\"><span class=\"stars staticStars notranslate\"><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p6\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p0\"><\/span><\/span> 3.76 avg rating &mdash; 1,653 ratings<\/span>            &mdash; published 2013\n          <\/div>\n\n    <div class=\"addBookTipDescription\">\n      \n<span id=\"freeTextContainer6854341542888021126\">The acclaimed author of The Wasted Vigil now gives us a searing, exquisitely written novel set in Pakistan and Afghanistan in the months following 9/11: a story of war, of one family’s losses, and of the simplest, most enduring human impulses. Jeo an<\/span>\n  <span id=\"freeText6854341542888021126\" style=\"display:none\">The acclaimed author of The Wasted Vigil now gives us a searing, exquisitely written novel set in Pakistan and Afghanistan in the months following 9/11: a story of war, of one family’s losses, and of the simplest, most enduring human impulses. Jeo and Mikal are foster brothers from a small town in Pakistan. Though they were inseparable as children, their adult lives have diverged: Jeo is a dedicated medical student, married a year; Mikal has been a vagabond since he was fifteen, in love with a woman he can’t have. But when Jeo decides to sneak across the border into Afghanistan—not to fight with the Taliban against the Americans, rather to help care for wounded civilians—Mikal determines to go with him, to protect him.  Yet Jeo’s and Mikal’s good intentions cannot keep them out of harm’s way. As the narrative takes us from the wilds of Afghanistan to the heart of the family left behind—their blind father, haunted by the death of his wife and by the mistakes he may have made in the name of Islam and nationhood; Mikal’s beloved brother and sister-in-law; Jeo’s wife, whose increasing resolve helps keep the household running, and her superstitious mother—we see all of these lives upended by the turmoil of war.  In language as lyrical as it is piercing, in scenes at once beautiful and harrowing, The Blind Man’s Garden unflinchingly describes a crucially contemporary yet timeless world in which the line between enemy and ally is indistinct, and where the desire to return home burns brightest of all.<\/span>\n  <a data-text-id=\"6854341542888021126\" href=\"#\" onclick=\"swapContent(\$(this));; return These were the points of the triangle forming the major route of the transatlantic slave trade. And these are the cities that acclaimed author Caryl Phillips explores--physically, historically, psychologically--in this wide-ranging meditation on the legacy of slavery and the impact of the African diaspora on the life of a place and its people.In a brilliantly layered narrative, Phillips combines his own observations with the stories of figures from the past. The experiences of an African trader in nineteenth-century Liverpool are contrasted with Phillips\'s experience of the city, where, as a Carib-bean black, he is scorned by the city\'s &quot;native&quot; blacks. His interactions with American Pan-Africanists coming &quot;home&quot; to Ghana (and with those Ghanaians for whom leaving seems the best hope) are paired with the account of a British-trained African minister in eighteenth-century Accra who turned a blind eye to the slave trade flourishing around him. The story of a white judge who disrupted &quot;the natural order&quot; in Charleston by integrating the Democratic primary in 1947 is set against Phillips\'s search for remnants of the &quot;pest houses&quot; where slaves were &quot;seasoned&quot; be-fore being sold.Phillips weaves these narrative threads together with acute insight and a novelist\'s grasp of time, place and character. The result is a provocative and unexpected book, at once historically illuminating and profoundly affecting.<\/span>\n  <a data-text-id=\"13383765205432720659\" href=\"#\" She retraces the history of the Atlantic slave trade from the fifteenth to the twentieth century and reckons with the blank slate of her own genealogy.There were no survivors of Hartman\'s lineage, nor far-flung relatives in Ghana of whom she had come in search. She traveled to Ghana in search of strangers. The most universal definition of the slave is a stranger--torn from kin and country. To lose your mother is to suffer the loss of kin, to forget your past, and to inhabit the world as a stranger. As both the offspring of slaves and an American in Africa, Hartman, too, was a stranger. Her reflections on history and memory unfold as an intimate encounter with places--a holding cell, a slave market, a walled town built to repel slave raiders--and with people: an Akan prince who granted the Portuguese permission to build the first permanent trading fort in West Africa; an adolescent boy who was kidnapped while playing; a fourteen-year-old girl who was murdered aboard a slave ship.Eloquent, thoughtful, and deeply affecting, Lose Your Mother is a powerful meditation on history, memory, and the Atlantic slave trade.<\/span>\n  <a data-text-id=\"8731920457888509904\"       <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/168076.Kamila_Shamsie">About Kamila Shamsie</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>
<a href="/author/show/168076.Kamila_Shamsie"><div class='bookAuthorProfile__photo' style='background-image: url(https://images.gr-assets.com/authors/1264510720p3/168076.jpg);'></div>
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__widgetContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__name'>
<a href="/author/show/168076.Kamila_Shamsie">Kamila Shamsie
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__followerCount'>
1,483
followers
</div>

</div>
</div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer8616152163303334328">Kamila Shamsie was born in 1973 in Karachi, where she grew up. She has a BA in Creative Writing from Hamilton College in Clinton, NY and an MFA from the University of Massachusetts, Amherst. While at the University of Massachusetts she wrote <i>
  <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/524894.In_The_City_By_The_Sea' title='In The City By The Sea' rel='nofollow noopener'>In The City By The Sea</a>
</i>, published by Granta Books UK in 1998. This first novel was shortlisted for the John Llewelyn Rhys Award in the UK, and Shamsie rec</span>
  <span id="freeText8616152163303334328" style="display:none">Kamila Shamsie was born in 1973 in Karachi, where she grew up. She has a BA in Creative Writing from Hamilton College in Clinton, NY and an MFA from the University of Massachusetts, Amherst. While at the University of Massachusetts she wrote <i>
  <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/524894.In_The_City_By_The_Sea" title="In The City By The Sea" rel="nofollow noopener">In The City By The Sea</a>
</i>, published by Granta Books UK in 1998. This first novel was shortlisted for the John Llewelyn Rhys Award in the UK, and Shamsie received the Prime Minister’s Award for Literature in Pakistan in 1999. Her 2000 novel <i>
  <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/290382.Salt_and_Saffron" title="Salt and Saffron" rel="nofollow noopener">Salt and Saffron</a>
</i> led to Shamsie’s selection as one of Orange’s “21 Writers of the 21st Century.” With her third novel, <i>
  <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/290384.Kartography" title="Kartography" rel="nofollow noopener">Kartography</a>
</i>, Shamsie was again shortlisted for the John Llewelyn Rhys award in the UK. Both <i>Kartography</i> and her next novel, <i>
  <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/290389.Broken_Verses" title="Broken Verses" rel="nofollow noopener">Broken Verses</a>
</i>, won the Patras Bokhari Award from the Academy of Letters in Pakistan. Burnt Shadows, Shamsie’s fifth novel, has been longlisted for the Orange Prize for Fiction. Her books have been translated into a number of languages.<br /><br />Shamsie is the daughter of literary critic and writer <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/221066.Muneeza_Shamsie" title="Muneeza Shamsie" rel="nofollow noopener">Muneeza Shamsie</a>, the niece of celebrated Indian novelist <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/135518.Attia_Hosain" title="Attia Hosain" rel="nofollow noopener">Attia Hosain</a>, and the granddaughter of the memoirist Begum Jahanara Habibullah. A reviewer and columnist, primarily for the Guardian, Shamsie has been a judge for several literary awards including The Orange Award for New Writing and The Guardian First Book Award. She also sits on the advisory board of the Index on Censorship. <br /><br />For years Shamsie spent equal amounts of time in London and Karachi, while also occasionally teaching creative writing at Hamilton College in New York State. She now lives primarily in London.</span>
  <a data-text-id="8616152163303334328" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>
